more or less equal, give or take some experimental and statistical noise. So, if you take the QFT of a single computational basis state, the result is an equal superposition of all states. If you're familiar with Fourier transforms, this probably doesn't surprise you. One basic principle that can help us build an intuitive connection between a function and its Fourier transform is that the width of a function is inversely proportional to the width of its Fourier transform. So, something that is very localized in time, for example, like a very short pulse, will require a broad range of frequencies to generate that pulse.
